"Programmes shall include the following activities: repair and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investments in cross-border waste management systems, medical facilities, research centres, flood measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or the development of tourist objectives, development of joint services for the local population, employment advice, the creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the Programme is provided on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of the cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has ended.
The funds shall be provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four characteristics: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing.'
Within the framework of the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of the project activities are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: joint selection of the lead partner, joint preparation of the project and application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, taking over the overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, total payments of support accepted by the lead partner and transferring them to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects carried out in the territory of the regions and voivodeship adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2005, the Council adopted Joint Action The European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation czech republic – Republic of Poland 2007-2013 (OPPS CR-POL).
€258,187,467 has been earmarked for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total amount included a contribution from the European Funds (ERDF) totalling €219,459,344 (85%), which must be spent by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the implementation of EU funds from the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The Agreement on the Cooperation of the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both offices on 27 December 2004. In September 2012, following previous negotiations at the Heads of SAEs of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia held in Lovasberen, Hungary, on 25 February 2013.
The aim of the cooperation was to verify whether the implementation of the operational programme is carried out in accordance with the legislation and with binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system is functioning reliably, whether the objectives of the operational programme are achieved and whether the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the specified conditions of the aid granted.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel control, in which the control authorities independently examined, on the basis of their statutory mandate, topics the content of which had been jointly discussed in advance.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit carried out also included elements of the performance audit.
The selected topics were examined on a sample of projects with project partners on both sides of the border.
The checks carried out resulted in control conclusions published normally by both authorities.
On the basis of the two control conclusions set out in the Annex, this joint report was produced.
The selected joint control sample included six projects (Annex 1) which fall under aid 1.1 — Strengthening transport accessibility and support area 2.2 — Promoting the development of tourism.
Prior to the start of the inspection, the use of funds from these two areas of support accounted for more than 60 % of the funds paid to beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects throughout the OPPS CR-POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to checking the joint sample of six projects, the SAO checked the other 13 projects and NIK checked two other projects (all from the support area 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the beneficiaries of ERDF support, which were controlled by the SAO and NIK, totalled €25,362,174 (SAO) and €8,450,784 (NIK).
The amount of funds audited represented 41 % of all appropriations paid to beneficiaries of aid from areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both independently carried out by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of the irregularities identified did not exceed the level of materiality.
The implementation of OPPS ČR–POL, which includes the operation of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The audited persons who were part of the management and control system have taken steps to reduce the risk of misuse of funds and the non-use of erdf funds.
Proof of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the stated objectives, which was also supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects.
SUMMARY OF THE RESULTS OF THE INSPECTION
A. Evaluation of the control and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to carry out a check of the control and control system of OPPS ČR–POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry of Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry of Regional Development pol) and the controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodeship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit made it possible to evaluate the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the review focused on activities carried out in connection with the setting and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in relation to the setting of the objectives of the programme and the monitoring of the progress made, the evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the implementation of the programme's funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies , together with the control activities of the managing authority, the setting of activities and the results from the audits of the audit body and the system of reporting and monitoring of irregularities and its compliance, coordination of tasks and cooperation between the institutions involved in the OPPS CR-POL was also verified.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the field of risk analysis and the drawing up of control plans at the site of project implementation were examined and the evaluation of project applications carried out by independent evaluators was assessed.
The verification of the control system included a review of the administrative and on-the-spot checks which allowed the approval of e.g. the control system. the supply of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, and at the same time the compliance of the disbursement of such funds and the related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant deficiencies were identified by the SAO's control in the area of publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies provided.
In the area of evaluation and approval of projects, the risk of non-transparency of this process was identified.
Other shortcomings were identified in the area of setting up monitoring indicators and in the setting of the irregularity reporting system.
On the basis of these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities detected by NIK concerning the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to infringements of the laws and rules in force were not assessed as significant.
These irregularities had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a material impact on the implementation of the whole OPPS CR–POL.
Despite the identified irregularities, NIK evaluated the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The check did not show a discrepancy between the indicators and the objectives set for each project and those defined by the operational programme.
In all audited projects, the expected results were achieved, the activities financed were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the operational programme project applications were met.
The conclusions are in line with the result of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of projects described below in the text.
An important aspect, which the SAO and NIK have addressed in their conclusions, is the limited possibility of objectively assessing the implementation of the project expected results with regard to the objectives of individual projects as well as the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On the initiative of the Supreme Control Chamber, an opinion poll was conducted in the summer of 2013 at the place of implementation of projects selected for the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation was carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nyse (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the investigation was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both to the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project carried out by Polish project partners and the Czech public in relation to the part of the project implemented in the Czech Republic.
The survey mainly concerned the suitability, quality, exchange of information, the use of funds and the social function of projects, including investments implemented.
The number of respondents for each project is set out in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall social evaluation of the projects was good and was 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that the results of the projects were positively perceived by the majority of respondents both with regard to the objectives and according to other parameters given in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the projects examined met the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noticed the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourist infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, the quality of life of citizens and the restoration of contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of achievement of the targets/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level expected by the respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of the participants in the panel discussions (target groups) carried out under the NIK control on the Polish side, who pointed out the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive public acceptance and the fulfilment of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of the legality of controlled projects
For the beneficiaries, the compliance of the project in kind and time with the schedule and content of the project application was verified, the fulfilment of the objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, the management of changes in projects, the management of any project revenue, the fulfilment of the obligation to separate accounting of project transactions, compliance with the prohibition of financing of projects from other EU sources, the implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions , the fulfilment of mandatory publicity concerning, in particular, the dissemination of awareness of the sources of EU project funding, compliance with the eligibility conditions for project expenditure, the selection of suppliers and the verification of the supplies delivered on the basis of the public contracts awarded.
The total financial value of the deficiencies of qualified SAO and NIK as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found at €85 094.
However, this amount did not exceed the established materiality level relating to the total amount of funds audited for the sample of projects.
Despite the irregularities found, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of the selected projects positively to the beneficiaries.
In most cases, the obligations resulting from the conditions of the aid were properly fulfilled by the beneficiary.
The funds for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU and national law.
However, seven of the eight beneficiaries of the aid audited by NIK were found to be irregularities in the fulfilment of material and financial commitments.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and concerned particularly inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, but which did not affect the choice of supplier or the supply of contractually agreed works.
In only one of all NIK-controlled projects, the irregularity found concerning non-compliance with reporting obligations can be assessed as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which EUR 8 231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure of CZK 2,098,053 (of which €76,863 financed by the ERDF) in three of the 19 projects audited.
Irregularities included the failure to deliver the material paid and the work or expense of upgrading the property owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary or as a project partner.
Recommendations
The JOINT RECOMMENDATION of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, in particular with a view to consistently establishing sound monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the operational programme priority axis.
In general, it is also necessary to seek to establish such indicators at programme level as well as projects, which will allow for a more reliable verification of the fulfilment of the identified impacts.
The notes and recommendations resulting from the checks carried out on both sides of the border are included in the SAO and NIK's separate cd control conclusions.
Communiqué
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the Presidents of the Supreme Control Institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public procurement and corruption prevention
Joint report on parallel control carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
The award of public contracts for goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and the private sector.
The role of the State is to make the most efficient and economical use of public resources and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor for the success of the Single Economic Area.
The EU has set limits in its rules, exceeding which the contracting authority must meet certain requirements to guarantee a broad and unhindered competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose european procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts that are valued below EU limits, the regulation of their award remains within the scope of individual countries.
Moreover, public procurement is prone to corruption, with a subsequent high potential for damage.
Therefore, contracting entities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair favoritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI – SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI – BRH) agreed to carry out parallel controls in the field of procurement procedures and to prevent corruption in land and transport buildings.
Cooperation was also focused on the area of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
The controls also included procurement below European limits in order to cover the area of corruption prevention.
The control findings of the two SAIs are summarised in a joint report addressed, inter alia, to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and controlled institutions
The BRH evaluation is based on area checks of the above mentioned areas, which were carried out by the German building authorities responsible for federal transport and construction between 2011 and 2012.
For example, highway construction and large-scale construction projects were evaluated.
The BRH included the findings of past controls in the evaluation.
The SAO's evaluation is based on inspections carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which were focused on road constructions, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal procurement is mostly carried out by the provincial building authorities.
In the Czech Republic, the state institution for road and rail construction is responsible for the award of public contracts in the field of transport infrastructure.
In the field of civil engineering, public procurement is carried out by the beneficiaries of budgetary appropriations.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used at the various stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation takes into account the differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the course of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and statutory standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany shall be governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts .
Internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details on the requirements of european procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called 'european procurement law'. procurement manuals of the federal government, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail in the light of legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation concerning contracts with value above and below European limits.
The procurement law undertouched substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up space for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during the procurement procedure.
Fundamental aspects of the procurement law in both countries
As regards European procurement procedures, open and narrower procedures must be applied in both countries in particular.
In Germany, contracts under the European limit are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, limited call for participation, negotiated procedures), but these are practically identical to the above conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of the preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and when called to participate in a competition, any candidate may submit an offer.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized companies.
This is achieved mainly by dividing the total public contract into commercially specific parts and by dividing it into sub-contracts.
The award of contracts divided into these parts gives the opportunity to participate in the proceedings to as many candidates as possible.
The two characteristics of the German procurement law are time-tested tools for obtaining good value for money.
More than 330,000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees are registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic, the conditions of European procedures must also be applied in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
The exception is a simplified sub-limit procedure where the contracting authority can use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, the Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
The current Czech legislation allows contracting entities to choose between open procedures and narrower procedures.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
The total volume of contracting authority contracts for public works and the share of the different types of procedures in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction work in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
czk, of which CZK 1,138 billion was CZK (15%) the proportion of public contracts, which, in addition to contracts awarded by the federal government, included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of projects of federal road and construction projects (excluding the construction of railways and water works) amounted to EUR 212.5 billion. Czk.
In 2011, the composition of federal contracts for land works and transport works was as follows: 80,7 % open management and public calls; 12.7% narrower management and narrower challenges;
6,6 % of negotiated procedures and direct procurement; 33.2% is the share of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic in 2011 amounted to EUR 118.6 billion. czk , which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction work.
In 2009, it amounted to EUR 194.0 billion. 37% of the total.
Structure of procurement types in 2011: 65,1 % open procedures; 14.3 % narrower procedure; 9,5 % of the negotiated procedure; 8,4 % simplified procedures for sub-limit contracts; 2.7% other proceedings.
Those figures shall include all public procurement contracts for supplies, services and works awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
The figures show that: parallel controls in both countries have focused on a substantial part of public procurement procedures; corruption prevention, which must still be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open meaning. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of the main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel controls on the award of European works contracts, with an emphasis on preventing corruption in their countries.
The controls were aimed at comparing legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public procurement in the field of land and transport constructions.
The following are key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national rights of both countries (paragraphs 1 and 3.1).
The prevention of corruption is not only the aim of the EU directive, but the national procurement rules of both countries, which differ in part in terms of focus and in detail.
In both countries, the basic requirement that the tender documents must meet is to include all the information needed to prepare tenders.
The specifications shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements limiting competition.
Tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is the lack of definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, the division of contracts into parts is required where possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to brh findings, this requirement is being complied with in Germany.
Sao's checks have shown that in the Czech Republic, very large public works in the field of road constructions are commissioned, without being divided into parts, or divided, but only in a very significant part of it. This method can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
Such constraints on market potential may result in a significant increase in costs and create a risk of non-serious pricing practices and inadmissible conventions.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting entities to subsequently enter additional works under certain conditions if this need arises only after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for the correct identification of the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, according to the applicable regulations, contracting entities can arbitrarily designate one of the open procurement procedures, namely an open procedure or a narrower procedure.
German rules in the procurement and budgetary law give priority to open procedures for both European tenders and national, known as public calls for competition.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a larger number of candidates to submit tenders and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it difficult to negotiate prices illegally between bidders.
At the same time, the best results are achieved in terms of value for money.
In the case of large contracts whose estimated total price reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, the contracting entities audited have always awarded European tenders.
The correct determination of the expected value of the contract plays a very important role in determining the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used to objectively determine the price of public works, in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the area of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish preliminary information and notices of the opening of the procurement procedure in order to increase competition by allowing candidates to prepare in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
When selecting candidates to participate in a shortlisted procurement procedure, there is a greater risk of manipulation.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee real competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and shall be published well in advance.
In addition to EU requirements, contracting entities in both countries should take turns as often as possible to encourage candidates to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are partly very detailed requirements for the prevention of manipulation risks.
In some cases, the contracting entities audited did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions have not always been credible (section 4.4).
In both countries, contracting entities must ensure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information during the procurement procedure.
Any agreement between those candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of specifications shall be prevented as far as possible in order to avoid any reduction in competition in the form of unlawful agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the German contracting authorities audited did not comply with that requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality requirements of tenders are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (section 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of tenders is procedurally regulated in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive, but have not always been respected by the contracting authorities under review.
For example, contracting entities forwarded their tenders to third parties for inspection without prior consideration without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
Thus, the tenders were insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulations (section 4.7).
The intended award of the contract must be discussed by the participants for the sake of legal protection, enterprises are to be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule also is designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The procurement documents were not always complete or entirely credible for the contracting authorities audited in both countries.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level have not always been properly respected in the management minutes.
In addition, some of the shortcomings identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (point 4.9).
Summary of the guiding principles of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption
European Procurement Law
The European Parliament and the Council of the European Union have adopted, inter alia, a directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works, supplies and services in order to ensure the free movement of goods and services and a directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to review procedures for the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (Review Procedures Directive) , the two directives were to be transposed into national law.
Eu general procurement principles include, in particular: a competitive environment requirement; the requirement of equal treatment or non-discrimination; transparency requirement.
In addition, eu procurement principles include the Directive on the coordination of public procurement procedures, including provisions on construction projects whose total estimated value is or exceeds EUR 5 million. €.
These provisions affect: the content of the tender documents; the type of procurement procedure used; disclosure of certain information before, during and after the proceedings; compliance with the deadline for receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that may be required of candidates; selection criteria for the most appropriate bid.
The legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Review Procedure Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of the procurement law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the examination procedures.
Both countries transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Prevention of corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
In either country, there is no legal definition of what corruption actually means.
In general, corruption is seen as an abuse of personal (confidential) status for its own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is incited by those who benefit.
Corruption can hinder competition and cause serious economic losses.
It is estimated that the total corruption-related damage is 5% of the world's economic output.
For the EU region, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved in the contract, conventions restricting competition or allowing dishonest conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, capacity and overall work of the public administration.
Therefore, the prevention of corruption is an essential task of the contracting authorities.
Practice and procedures must be checked, in particular in areas particularly prone to corruption, in order to take adequate measures to prevent it.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective controls can substantially reduce the susceptibility of the decision-making process to corruption.
The award of works contracts is generally considered to be particularly prone to corruption.
Measures in the field of corruption prevention
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, which is particularly relevant to active and passive bribery, the recruitment and offering of significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual with recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the federal administration and according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process outside the risk analysis: the preference for open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; separation of the planning, procurement and accounting phases; the precise and complete documentation of the procurement procedure.
In addition, another preventive action against corruption has become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system for monitoring the procurement of federal roads.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, the total value of the contract and the involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed to identify possible irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, as is the case with the level of labour or civil law.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and non-discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set out by the government in the Government's Strategy against Corruption for the period 2011 to 2012, which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of these measures were incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and subsequent implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: lowering the limits for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notification by the contracting authority, including the justification of the contract; a ban on limiting the number of tenderers in a restricted procedure and abolishing the general possibility of limiting the number of applicants by drawing a draw; the obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; the obligation to publish actual and subcontracting lists.
Results of controls relating to subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Tender documents
The tender documents shall contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate form.
In particular, the documentation shall allow the tender price to be processed.
In order to do so, tenderers must not be exposed to risks they could not count on.
The tender documents contain, in particular, specifications, the requirements for proving the qualifications of the tenderers and the criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
European law contains, in particular, requirements relating to technical specifications.
Specifications must minimise competition and all interested parties must have equal access to them.
Specifications must be neutral.
Requirements for specific specific products are permissible on a very limited scale in the procurement documents.
The basic principle of corruption prevention is that the tender documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid ambiguity or inaccurate data that create scope for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications are generally given without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications shall specify the required supplies and services and specify in detail the number of units of work and supplies.
In normal cases, prices should be quoted by units of work or supplies (e.g. in CZK/m or IN CZK/item).
Defining the required work in terms of their functionality is permissible only in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that specifications need to be drawn up completely and unequivocally.
They must contain exact quantities and must not include works and supplies, the necessity of which is not entirely obvious.
Payment in the form of lump sums or according to the time worked shall be avoided where possible.
These parts of the procurement documents shall exclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must enable each tenderer to prepare his tender with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender must be made public only if the tender documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for the contractor to award construction contracts in parts.
The contracting authority may divide the contract into several separate parts, provided that the nature of the subject-matter of such a contract so permits.
In Germany, the application of the rule to divide works into parts is required, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific aspects (specialised parts of the contract).
This allows even SMEs to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential applicants and makes it difficult to fix prices.
This kind of input is usually considered the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, the tender documents must contain evaluation criteria in national and European procurement procedures.
This should inform the tenderer comprehensively and exhaustively of the criteria according to which the tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to indicate the evaluation criteria in the notification applies only to European calls for tender.
In national procurement procedures, it is necessary to include the evaluation criteria only in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
When tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion of any information that would help identify specific companies, architects or designers.
This is to prevent individual construction companies from obtaining any additional information or other undesues competitive advantages without the contracting authority's knowledge, thanks to contacts with these independent experts.
This is also why independent experts are not allowed to publish tender documents and make them accessible for screening.
The contracting authority is the only point of contact for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, after the conclusion of the original contract, there is almost always the need for more work or modification of existing works.
The legislation of both countries allows the same supplier to award a multi-work contract within a certain limit without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of multi-work under European and German legislation must not exceed 50 % of the original value of the contract.
In addition, more work must be necessary and must be of the nature of a specific part of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied to multi-work.
This may risk a greater time delay in the implementation of the contract and the possible integration of a new entity into an already running project.
Where a multi-work contract is awarded to a supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision that prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified work or multiple works.
Calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to more work, there may be less work during construction, i.e. less work. work that was not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less work and more work cannot be compensated for.
Main control findings
Bhr
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In rare cases, specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but they were replaced by EU standards in the meantime.
In some cases, the specifications contained data requiring a particular trade mark, e.g. for the system of resistance of public inscriptions, for the prevention of water penetration or for noise-cancelling compresses, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into items
In a single case, the BRH did not notice a non-conformity with the general requirements for the call for supply and service tenders according to the quantitative specifications broken down into headings.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, tender documents were drawn up in full before the invitation to tender was published.
However, in various cases, documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in the items or timetable had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, the tender documents contained work and supplies, the need of which was uncertain.
One tender document for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of the contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for notdivising the supplies was not demonstrated.
For the criteria for evaluating tenders before the award of the contract, BRH did not find any shortcomings.
Inclusion of the names of authorized independent experts
Many of the documents included interested authorised experts.
The names were given on the opening pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact an explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the procurement procedure.
For construction projects, these cases were found mainly in the procurement documents, where self-employed experts were entrusted with the processing of specifications.
Contracting entities stated that they did not have sufficient staffing capacity to closely examine the tender documents.
Sao
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 apartments, the reference was directly to the specific manufacturers and brands of products.
The contracting authority did not accept the use of other similar types of products or to justify the request for the supply of specific products.
For the construction of the university's facilities, the tender documents contained references to specific business companies and specific product designations.
Overall overview of specifications and sub-specifications
For the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the procurement documents the quantity and type of work, supplies or services required in the acreage statement and set only the built-in space in cubic metres as a unit of measure for each building.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the road circuit, the tender documents for the narrower procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation caused by the extension of the scope of the contract and the change in technical conditions.
In the construction of social buildings, the tender documents had defects that forced the creation of multiple works (lack of precise focus, insufficient structural technical survey, lack of drawings).
In the tender documents for the construction of a large library, the interior elements were listed only descriptively, without specification of the quality requirements for products.
The supply of interior equipment of the building was provided by subcontractors, who acquired some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for awarding a contract
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation to award a contract in parts.
The analysis of three major road works during the construction of the road circuit showed that savings of up to 790 mil could be achieved when commissioned in open-drive in parts (separately after selected buildings). Czk 15,508 million of the public procurement price achieved. Czk.
For example, the award of six buildings as a separate part of the public contract could have achieved a saving of EUR 62.5 million.
Czk, i.e. czk 100,0 25 % of the contracted price and the award of 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract could achieve a saving of EUR 287,8 million. Czk, i.e. czk 100,0 also 25%.
When constructing roads, the contracting authority established qualification criteria in the procurement documents which limited the participation of small suppliers in the procurement procedure.
In the terms of the open procedure for the supplier for one construction of the motorway construction, the contracting authority requested, inter alia, the contracting authority to grant the contracting authority a contract for the construction of the motorway. the supplier's practice of implementing at least three road works with a financial volume of more than EUR 700 million each. completed or operational during the last five years.
In the Czech Republic there are only nine companies that meet the condition of completion of buildings over 700 mil. czk in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of other open-management motorway constructions included six separate buildings for which the contracting authority requested a minimum annual volume of construction work in the last three years of EUR 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. € 2.5 billion out of € 3.7 billion) in the period of 2008–2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of EUR 13.2 billion, the commission was responsible for the total of EUR 13.2 billion in 2004. There were only two or even one candidate.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential could lead to a large increase in costs.
Multi-work area
The contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of multiple works of construction during the construction of the road circuit and did not award any of the procedures specified in the act.
The value of the pre-approved multi-work in one case was EUR 1 591.4 million. Czk without VAT (increase of 21 %) and in the latter case 4 135,8 mil. czk without VAT (increase of 91 %).
The cause of the multi-work was mainly the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
In the construction of the higher education institution, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documents some works and supplies, the necessities of which he knew about the necessary for the completion and approval of the construction when the contract was awarded.
For these works and supplies he concluded additions to the contract for work with the contractor with an increase in the price, in the negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of multi-works exceeded the national limit of 20 % of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the palace of justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to enter additional works without a procurement procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by EUR 50.5 million. Czk.
The unit prices of additional works fixed without competition were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same supplier.
Other findings
On the selected sample of buildings, the SAO found that the average construction costs of building one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic are 355.0 mil. Czk.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany for a mileage of a four-lane motorway is 260.0 miles. Czk.
This comparison suggests that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions on tender documents are almost the case in both countries and comply with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and prevent corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow candidates to calculate costs and prepare their tenders.
In addition, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to commission more work.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are complemented by additional rules designed to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and for the implementation of manipulations.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, there is greater transparency achieved by the publication of the procurement criteria.
In both countries, the requirements for specifications are mostly met.
However, in some cases deficiencies have been identified which could lead to a reduction in competition.
At the same time, a rule in Force in Germany requiring the division of bulky construction projects into parts seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, the award of contracts for large-scale projects without awarding in parts or division into large volumes can limit the circle of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
The strict requirement for applicants' references, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of candidates for road works, is the fact that public contracts are awarded only to a small number of applicants, so that small and medium-sized companies do not have a chance to compete.
A limited number of applicants also increases the risk of price fixation and other illegal conventions.
As regards the award of additional contracts for more than work or modified work and their reimbursements, regulatory approaches are different in both countries.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50 % of the value of the original contract, the multi-work may be awarded without a procurement procedure and its notification.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authority.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the 20 % limit is exceeded, more work must be entered on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
By dividing the works into parts, it is possible to avoid the reduction of competition and the subsequent unfortunate consequences.
Determination of the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers who will be admitted to the competition.
The contracting authority may leave the range of potential suppliers unrestricted or limit the scope according to the specified criteria.
In the case of major project works, the value of which reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
When choosing a type of procedure, the contracting authority must in particular comply with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimate of the total cost of the contract for the required works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of EUR 5,0 million, the commission shall, in the absence of any other information, be required to provide the total cost of the contract with the required works. € (EUR 125 million) it is always required to open or restricted a procurement procedure at European level.
In exceptional cases, negotiated procedures or competitive dialogue shall be admissible.
Where a construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may, to a limited extent, derogate from the European procurement procedure by asking for a national procedure if the total value of the part concerned is below the limit of EUR 1,0 million. € (EUR 25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the value of the estimate of the whole contract.
Dividing a construction project or deliberately estimating its costs too low with the intention of avoiding a fundamental obligation to issue a European procurement procedure is not permissible.
The choice of the type of management is very important for the prevention of corruption, as the restriction of competition significantly increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, an important part of the procurement procedure documentation is a detailed justification for the type of procedure chosen, in particular in relation to the obligation to application of the European procurement law.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures as well as for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of proceedings are allowed only if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech regulation, the relevant German regulation of budgetary law and the principles of corruption prevention prefers open procedures or a public invitation to participate in proceedings.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted proceedings, shall be permitted only exceptionally if permitted by law.
Such exceptions shall be justified and documented.
The principle of open management preference is intended to ensure that restrictions on competition are avoided to the maximum extent and that the opportunity for manipulation (selected by specific candidates in advance) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is at the heart of the prevention of corruption.
A correct estimate of the expected price of the contract is an important basis for the decision on the corresponding type of procurement procedure.
When estimating the cost of construction projects, contracting entities in Germany can rely on budget and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These were compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for types of buildings, building units, building parts and partial works.
Based on such cost baselines, it is possible to estimate the value of the contract of the planned project or construction work.
Only a few construction administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded at national level.
Contracting entities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable buildings to determine the expected costs of public contracts for construction sites.
In many cases, the cost determination is based on an estimate by the entity that finances the project or is based on the value of the resources available to that entity.
The standardisation measures are still at an early stage.
On the basis of the anti-corruption programme for the period 2011 to 2012, the relevant ministries are to draw up a database of the usual prices of standardised commodities.
The application of the price system for determining the expected price of the contract is determined by a new Regulation ordering the subject matter of the contract to be determined in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly works, materials and products and other data in order to calculate the necessary costs and to determine unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport constructions for the purpose of their inspection, which are regularly updated.
The database is intended for determining the price of the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of the preparation of the works.
Key BRH control findings
Selection of the procurement procedure
The contracting entities audited generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public call for participation, thus complying with the requirements of budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal construction projects in the field of civil engineering was 59.5%.
In road construction, this proportion was 94,8 %.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of a blanket check, BRH found, on the basis of information collected from more than 16 000 proceedings, that limited management has a clear disadvantage compared to public management and public challenge, and limited management also increases the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, the tender prices in the restricted procedure and the negotiated procedure were between 6 % and 10 % higher than in public proceedings.
Parallel studies of the Ministry of Construction and the Provincial Control Court confirmed differences in value for money raised in public and limited management.
A study by the Ministry of Construction even found that prices in limited management were twice as high as those in public proceedings.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total contract value was estimated at more than EUR 5 million, the total value of the contract was estimated at eur 5 million. € (EUR 125 million) as a rule, the contracting entities audited prepared tender plans specifying which works would be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the tender plans, the proportion of works commissioned under European rules always exceeded 80% of the total work.
There was no case of circumvention of the european procedure requirement by means of an excessively low cost estimate.
However, in some cases, the minimum share for pan-European management was not reached as a result of the calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the work, some contracting authorities have illegally changed the procurement plan.
They did not justify these amendments and did not demonstrate that the minimum share of the European procedure would subsequently be respected.
As a general rule, the share of European procedures under project accounts was lower than that set out in the procurement plan.
If the expected values of construction project contracts exceeded European limits, the contracting entities did not use the European procedure for all the individual sub-sections in value exceeding EUR 1 million. € (EUR 25 million) (czk 100,
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving valid reasons for the need for a derogation from the requirement.
Sao
Selection of the type of procurement procedure
In the case of several major construction projects (e.g. road section construction, equipment for a major sporting event and the construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The division reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded in a less demanding mode or even outside the law.
Methods of calculating the estimated price
The price norms applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective tool for determining the projected price.
They were based on the prices mentioned in the completed proceedings, but they were affected by both wastefulness and excessive profit by suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for the three parts of the road were about 37 % lower than these price norms.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the norms in relation not only to prices from the past, but also in comparison with other comparable markets and price developments.
For building constructions, the estimated price was based on the project budget carried out by architects or on the investor's estimate.
The actual price then differed considerably from the projected price.
The estimated cost of building a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but increased by EUR 54 million during the implementation. Czk, i.e. czk 100,0 by 27%.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33 % lower than the estimated price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the estimated price and increased even higher during the implementation process.
It was about 400 miles in the construction of a large library. Czk, i.e. czk 100,0 by 22%. For the construction of a palace of justice, the actual price increased disproportionately during the construction process (by 139 %) compared to the increase in functional areas (only by 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of type of procurement procedure and on respect for European limits in their national legislation, so that the relevant national adjustments of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public proceedings in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national arrangements in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public invitation to participate gives more competitors the opportunity to participate in the procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of tenders that guarantee the best value for money effect.
The prevention of corruption is significantly strengthened, as the circle of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which thus gives scope for manipulation and fixation of prices between tenderers.
The studies confirmed the benefits of the German provision on the preference for open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech legislation allows contracting entities to award contracts in an open or restricted procedure.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a ban on contracting authorities from limiting the number of tenderers in restricted procedures.
The closer procedure has now come very close to an open procedure.
In Germany, the contracting authority is largely satisfied with the requirement of an open procedure preference or a public call for competition, whereas in the Czech Republic some projects have been divided in breach of the law, in order to circumvent the requirement to select the relevant type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of some projects carried out in Germany, european limits were not respected, resulting in the failure to achieve a minimum share in pan-European procedures.
A satisfactory system has not yet been put in place in the Czech Republic to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, by means of more extensive controls, that the requirements for the selection of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met or they should create the conditions necessary to enable the expected costs to be calculated correctly.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
In cases where contracts are awarded under a European procedure, the contracting authority shall publish a contract report.
Preliminary information and contract notices are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
By means of advance notifications, the contracting authority shall indicate the basic specifications of the intended projects.
Preliminary notifications are required for projects for which the estimate of total costs is equal to or greater than the European limit and when the contracting authority wishes to shorten the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority shall publish a contract notice with detailed information on all public contracts requiring a European procedure.
The information shall be binding on the contracting authority after publication.
Among other things, the notification shall contain specifications, time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weights.
A common procurement vocabulary must be used to describe the subject-matter of the contract.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU's Official Journal and on the sponsor's profile.
In order to prevent discrimination against foreign applicants, notifications at national level should not contain any additional information and should not be published before the text of the notification to the EU Publications Office is sent.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in the prior notification may violate competition or even facilitate manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU provisions on prior information and procurement notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notifications must be published even in the case of contracts for which European management is not required.
The procurement procedure shall not be opened earlier than one month after the prior notification has been sent.
Prior information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, preliminary information is not required in cases where european procedure is not necessary.
In the case of national proceedings, the public invitation must be published on the federal government's website and, if appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted proceedings without competition from the parties, the notification shall be published where the estimated price of the contract is equal to or greater than € 25 000 (625 ths. (czk 100,
Preliminary announcements must explain all planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small orders in Germany.
Dividing projects into smaller parts is not so common in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Pre-notification
Contracting entities published a preliminary notification of projects which included works to be commissioned in the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they shortened the deadline, although the number of days between sending a pre-notification and sending a contract notification was less than 52 days.
In the case of other abbreviated procedures, the annual periods applicable to the provisional information have expired and should have been renewed.
In many cases, the prior notification did not indicate the envisaged division of the works into several parts.
Contract notice
Some notifications contained codes from the common dictionary that departed from the content of the pre-notification or did not specify the exact work to be carried out.
In rare cases, notifications at national level included more information, e.g. terms or published before the notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority has hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It published a notice on its platform, the use of which was charged.
However, the contracting authority did not check whether the company had to comply with the requirement to send further notices to a free internet government portal.
The sample found that only a third of the notifications were actually published on the government portal.
Sao
Contract notice
In the construction of the waste water treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set criteria in the notification of a shortlisted procurement procedure to the contractor for the construction, according to which it would reduce a large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The method of selecting (reducing) the evaluation tenders was not able to be demonstrated by the contracting authority.
Previous legislation allowed for a reduction in the number of offers.
In the construction of the road circuit, the contracting authority did not specify in the notice of initiation of a restricted procedure or in the qualification documentation the values according to which it will assess the level of fulfilment of the qualification criteria or the number of candidates to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the fulfilment of the qualification in such a way that all six candidates in the shortlist met it, but excluded one of them, having fulfilled only the last sixth place.
The contracting authority's procedure was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on what of the qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of calls
In the 1990s, the contracting authority did not publish a written invitation to submit tenders in the former military raids.
Conclusions and recommendations / summary
The two-step information process with preliminary information and the publication of notices of initiation is an important tool for meeting the transparency requirements and strengthening the competitive environment applied in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany in cases of limited procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
In contrast to the open procedure, limited procurement procedures do not allow participation by all potential tenderers who would be interested.
In restricted procedures, the contracting authority first selects potential suppliers to be asked to submit a tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow the number of applicants in European restricted procedures to be limited.
However, a restricted procedure requires at least five tenderers to be invited to submit a tender and at least three potential tenderers must be invited in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently wide to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria must be objective and non-discriminatory and must be published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is very prone to manipulation, e.g. by the selection of potential candidates who will be invited. preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders can ease their secret deal.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control in order to ensure that the procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for the selection of candidates are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European Directive, contracting entities should amend tenderers who invite tenders to submit tenders.
It is also not permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting entities must document and justify the selection of tenderers and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany, at least three tenderers are required in the event of a restricted procedure and several suitable candidates in the case of negotiated procedures.
Except in two cases, the new Czech rules do not allow to limit the number of candidates addressed in the case of a closer and negotiated procedure.
In Germany, when selecting candidates, the 'four-eyed principle' must be respected.
While one worker proposes a list, another has to substantially modify the proposal, e.g. in the form of a list of the product. by deleting the proposed tenderer or by the introduction of another.
This is to prevent a situation where an individual can definitively determine the circle of tenderers who participate in the procedure.
If such a provision is not in place, a far-reaching illegal agreement between the parties shall be allowed.
The list of tenderers who will be invited to submit a tender shall be confidential and shall not be disclosed.
This also obliges authorized independent experts.
There is no similar measure in the Czech Republic, nor a measure for confidential treatment of the list of tenderers.
According to German provisions, they may not designate candidates from external companies, but they may make proposals to the contracting authority.
There is no similar requirement in the Czech Republic.
Main control findings
BRH
Number of leads
Apart from a few cases, the requirement to reach a minimum number of applicants has been met.
An analysis of almost 7 000 assignments for buildings with a value within the national limit showed that, in the case of restricted procedures, contracting entities invited an average of 6.6 potential tenderers and, in the case of direct negotiated procedures, an average of 4.4 tenderers.
Change of suppliers and regional restrictions
In particular, in the area of building construction, the contracting authorities did not change the circle of candidates addressed because they had good experience with those with whom they had cooperated so far.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that, in the case of non-public proceedings, they should guarantee that they would reach out to tenderers whose suitability they had already assured.
Although there was no evidence for illegal regional restrictions, the non-public tenders included a greater proportion of regional applicants than there were in public proceedings.
The contracting authorities explained this by the low level of the contract compared to the public procedure and the high cost of travel for potential tenderers.
Cross-check principle and proposals of self-employed professionals
Contracting entities mostly ignored the obligation to change the list of candidates proposed by self-employed professionals.
In particular, they argued for staff shortages, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of a suitable candidate.
In many cases, the records did not show who suggested the tenderer who was asked to participate in the proceedings.
The examination of certain samples showed that the list of potential candidates was created by self-employed experts and was adopted without modification by the contracting authority.
Thus, the contracting authorities did not sufficiently protect themselves from potential manipulation.
Confidentiality of the treatment of lists of potential tenderers
One contracting authority kept a list of tenderers for invitations to the procurement procedure in a database to which all employees had access at any time.
This allowed the names of the envisaged or accepted tenderers to be manipulated or unauthorized to be disclosed, who could then agree on the price.
Documentation
The dossier provided reasons for the selection of candidates only in a few cases.
Sao
Criteria for the selection of candidates
During the construction of the road circuit, the interested parties did not have information on what data on their qualification would be evaluated and how.
For an action related to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports in the notification of the procurement procedure for participation in a restricted procedure, criteria have not been laid down to limit the number of tenderers.
All eight tenderers met the qualification requirements, but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority restricted participation in the procurement procedure.
The Commission then carried out the selection of tenderers by the method of random selection – by drawing, which was legal at the time.
This procedure should have already been indicated as a criterion in the contract notice.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in the selection of the winning tenderer in limited procurement procedures, the two countries have put in place special measures to prevent the illegal restriction of competition.
German rules limit such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called "black-and-green" manipulations. cross-checking (four-eye principle), by maintaining the confidentiality of the list of candidates and by prohibiting proposals for the selection of candidates to external companies.
The Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions with the aim of manipulation mainly by an unlimited number of applicants.
However, the check found that part of those rules had not been applied sufficiently and that an inadmissible restriction on the ring of candidates had not been prevented in some cases.
Contracting entities are obliged to completely eliminate these deficiencies.
Tender
